INTRODUCTION
During my stay in India (February-April, 1969) which I visited under the
Cultural Exchange Programme between the Governments of India and Cze-
choslovakia, I had a chance to see the well-known centre of Tibetan studies
in India, viz. International Academy of Indian Culture in New Delhi, and to
meet its Director, Professor Dr. Lokesh Chandra, who is also Editor of the
equally well-known Sata-Pitaka Series. I was able to tell him of the col-
lection of rare Tibetan books, manuscripts and blockprints preserved in
the library of the Oriental Institute of the Czechoslovak Academy of Scien-
ces, Prague', and especially the unique collection of 5,615 title leaves
(cover-prints) of various Tibetan works printed at the dGon-chen and the
dPal-spungs monasteries of Derge (sDe-dge) in Eastern Tibet. I myself was
able to acquire these title leaves for the Oriental Institute during my stay
in China, 1957-59. As it stands now, the collection represents an extremely
useful bibliographical guide for the study of Tibetan literature which, un-
fortunately, in general remains as unknown and inaccessible for us as Tibet
itself. Hearing of the Oriental Institute's collections, Professor Lokesh
Chandra who has himself contributed so considerably to the study of Ti-
betan literature by his invaluable Materials for a History of Tibetan Litera-
ture (ſata-Pitaka, vols. 28–30, New Delhi 1963), encouraged me to get the
title leaves published. Indeed it was basically owing to this conversation
that the idea of editing the present work took shape. During the second
half of 1969 the material was made ready for printing.
However, the proper story of our collection begins as early as the spring
of 1958. At that time, just in the middle of my two years of study at the
Central Academy of Nationalities (Chung-yang min-tsu hsüeh-yűan), Peking,
my teacher of Tibetan, Professor Yü Tao-ch'uan (Yu Dawchyuan) had
suggested to me to buy - in addition to the Kanjur and Tanjur collections
the purchase of which I had then been negotiating with the respective
authorities in Peking and in Derge - another set of some five thousand
1)
A detailed description of Tibetan holdings - both manuscript and
blockprinted - in the library of the Oriental Institute, Prague, is to
be found in my book, Tibetan Manuscripts and Blockprints in the
Library of the Oriental Institute Prague, Prague 1969.
The Prague collection of the Kanjur and Tanjur in 316 volumes (in-
cluding two Index-solumes), now kept in the library of the Oriental
Institute, was printed at the dGon-chen monastery of Derge, summer
1958. The whole set of books was sent to Prague on the 26th De-
cember, 1958. Cf, my "Notes on the Kanjur and Tanjur in Prague",
Archiv orientální 30 (Prague 1962), pp. 314-317.



10
and six hundred minor texts produced by the two monastic printing houses
in Derge, viz. the dGon-chen monastery and the dPal-spungs monastery.
dGon-chen or "Great Monastery” was once the capital of the Derge dis-
trict in the former Hsi-k'ang province, and now consists of a large Sa-skya-
opa monastery called IHun- grub-steng' and several other governmental
palaces. The principal building in the monastery is the printing establish-
ment, sDeodge par-khang chos-mdsod chen-mo, opened in the first half of
the eighteenth century during the illustrious reign of the King bs Tan-pa-
-tshe-ring of Derge (1678–1738), where the Kanjur and Tanjur collections,
as well as numerous gsung-'bums and bstan-bcos, canonical texts and
dhāranis, histories and rnam-thars, along with many other works on Budd-
hism in general (regardless of sectarian distinctions) have been printed. 4
dPal-spungs or "Heap of Glory” is a bKa'-rgyud-pa monastery near Derge,
founded in the me-lug or fire-sheep year, i.e. 17274 by Si-tu bs Tan-pa'i-
- nin-byed, the famous grammarian. The dPal-spungs printery is celebrated
for its excellent edition of the voluminous collection of Lamaist texts
called the Rin-chen gter-mdsod or "Repository of Jewels", compiled in the
nineteenth century at the order of Kong-sprul Rin-po-che Yon-tan-rgya-
-mtsho, as well as for printing the rŇing-ma-pa texts.
Naturally, Yü Tao-ch'uan's suggestion was a most attractive one, but the
cost of acquiring the complete texts of these works was too considerable
to be undertaken at that time. However, the authorities in Peking and in
Derge willingly complied with my wish and enabled me to buy at least the
separate title leaves of these works. The library of the Central Academy of
3)
Founded, according to a tradition, by Mahasiddha Thang-stong-rgyal-
-po, a sage who is said to have lived 125 years, in the middle of the
fifteenth century. Cf. J. Kolmaš, A Genealogy of the Kings of Derge
(Sde-dge'i rgyal-rabs), Prague 1968, pp. 31 et seq.
For a list of 143 prints of the dGon-chen Press, compiled after a
par-tho in possession of Rai Bahadur T.D. Den sapa of Sikkim, see
Lokesh Chandra, "Les imprimeries tibétaines de Drepung, Derge et
Pepung”, Journal asiatique 1961 (pp. 503-517), pp. 507-516. I was
also able to consult a MS. list of the dGon-chen prints from a copy of
the same in possession of Mr. E. Gene Smith in New Delhi. This MS.
copy is based, in its turn, on a list prepared for Professor R.A. Stein
of Paris. I would like to express my gratitude to Mr. E. Gene Smith
for having kindly lent me his copy.
5)
For a partial list of 18 prints of the DP al-spungs Press, see Lokesh
Chandra, op. cit., pp. 516-517. -. The further destiny of both print.
eries following the fateful year of 1959 remains unknown to me. Lo-
kesh Chandra, op. cit., p. 504, quotes a contemporary Tibetan refugee
scholar, Zimai (i.e. Dse-smad) Rin-po-che, according to whom "les
planches gravees de Derge ont été utilisées pour c: crire des ponts
ou brulées". If this is really so, as reported, the value of the title
leaves reproduced herewith will be all the greater.



11
Nationalities in Peking itself followed the same course; at that time it was
only the Peking National Library (Pei-ching kuo-li t'u-shu-kuan) which
ordered the complete set of these texts in order to deposit them with other
Tibetan holdings in the library's Fraternal Nationalities Department
(Hsiungoti min-tsu tsu). It was Professor Yü Tao-ch'uan who, besides work-
ing up a preliminary classification of the whole material (see below), also
suggested that identical numbering be employed between the texts kept in
Peking and the title leaves preserved in Prague to facilitate, in the future,
the borrowing of a pertinent work in the Peking National Library. These
title leaves, totalling 5,615 items, reached Prague in the spring of 1999 and
since then have been deposited in the Tibetan Section of the library of
the Oriental Institute. Only now, with the present edition, do Tibetanists
all over the world have the chance of a more detailed knowledge of the
collection.
As to the copies kept in the Peking National Library, unfortunately I am
not in a position to tell with certainty what happened to them during the sub-
sequent trouble. Nor is it known to me how far they have been further pro-
cessed, what new signatures – if any – they were provided with, or where
the prints are kept now, etc. The only surviving link between the two col-
lections in Peking and in Prague is in fact their common numerical de-
signation (Group numbers and Item numbers), which it thus became impera-
tive for the editor to preserve in the present edition (see the numerical
headings in the right upper corner of each even page).
Keeping constantly in mind the aforesaid identity in numerical class-
ification which obtains between the texts kept in Peking and the title
leaves preserved in Prague, it was necessary to arrange the present ma-
terial strictly and consistently according to this system, developed for the
author by Professor Yü Tao-ch'uan (in manuscript), and it has been applied
herewith with only few minor corrections which, however, do not alter in
any way the original system of consecutive numbers (all instances of my
further re-arrangement of Yü Tao-ch'uan's division of the material are stat-
ed explicitly in their proper places). Unfortunately, Yü Tao-ch'uan's sug-
gested arrangement of the material into volumes (or divisions) is often in-
correct or unsystematic (cf. for instance, the disorderly arrangement of the
Dwags po'i gsung 'bum, p. 22, or that of the Mi pham gyi gsung 'bum,
pp. 25 - 26, etc.). Similarly too, with some volumes there are often incor-
porated (by mistake?) items which, according to their side-captions, should
rather belong to separate volumes (see, for instance, vol. GA of the rGyud
sde kun btus, p. 18, into which have entered also titles marked with the
marginal letter KHA and NGA, and elsewhere). In several cases of the col-
lections of texts denoted by Yü Tao-ch'uan as IDeb thung yongs rdsogs
6)
The first bibliographical notice of these cover-prints being kept in
the library of the Oriental Institute, Prague, is to be found in my
Tibetan Manuscripts and Blockprints (see above, note 1), pp. 10–11.



(Small-size prints), IDeb 'bring gras (Medium-size prints), dee tshan sna
tshogs (Miscellaneous books), bs Deb chung (Small-format books),etc., it
is not at all clear which criteria have been applied in classifying the re-
spective texts. Although running the risk of presenting herewith an edition
that is insufficient in many respects, I have resolved to reprint the whole
material as it stands, without any omissions or additions, keeping as meti-
culously to Yü Tao-ch'üan's original arrangements as possible.
In the section Analysis of the Contents (below), I give a survey of the
nature of the texts reproduced, explaining their arrangement in the present
edition. With only the title leaves to consult, it was practically impossible
(or very difficult at least) to present more information on the character of
the respective works, their size, and other useful data concerning the
author/editor, place and year of edition, etc., usually to be gathered from
the colophon at the end of a Tibetan book. I have therefore collected only
the basic general data on the respective group of texts, its au thor/editor
(if known), number of volumes/divisions and the total number of indivi-
dual items. Also indicated is the original numerical marking (Prague/Pe-
king numbers) for the benefit of a potential user of this catalogue. More-
over, to facilitate the reference, all the texts have been provided with con-
secutive numbering (Serial nos. I to 5,615; cf. a similar system used by
Lokesh Chandra in his Materials for a History of Tibetan Literature). For
the sake of a mutual comparison and/or eventual completion of individual
items, I have decided to reproduce anew in the present edition also those
dkar-chags which have already been published (e.g. in Materials for a Hi-
story of Tibetan Literature, Catalogue of the Tohoku University Collection
of Tibetan Works on Buddhism, and elsewhere). This conceras the following
instances: Group nos. I, II, III, IV, XII, XVIII, and XX of the dGon-chen
collection and Group nos. IV-V of the dPal-spungs collection (see under
the respective headings).
The high quality of the photomechanic reproduction must be attributed
to maximum possible care on the part of the publisher. Nevertheless, in
several cases it suffers from an imperfect print of the original texts. In
consequence of this, a few of the title leaves are difficult to read or even
completely illegible. These shortcomings, it is hoped,, may be partially
remedied when the supplementary volume of this edition is eventually pu-
blished, giving an alphabetical list - in transcription - of all the titles
reproduced herewith with references to their respective Serial numbers in
the present catalogue,
In making the facsimiles we kept to the following procedure: on each
page appear no more than five title leaves with the cumulative designation
of their respective Prague numbers and Serial numbers (in italics) common
for the two opposite pages. The photographing, as a rule, was executed in
the scale 1:0.500 (coefficient 2). In those cases where, on account of the
size of some title leaves, it became necessary slightly to reduce the scale
of the texts reproduced, a comparative scale (in cm.) has always been in-
T



dicated. In eight instances (Prague nos. 02.121, 16.288, 18.017; 20.806,
22.067, 22.091, 22.159, and 50.221), in which the title leaves are wanting
in our collection, this has been indicated by leaving an empty space on
the respective place with a note "Missing”. In order to distinguish them
easily, the separate groups always start on a new page.
Before closing, I should like to express my gratitude to Professor Yü
Tao-ch'uan, professor of Tibetan in the Central Academy of Nationalities
in Peking, and to all individuals and institutions concerned for kindly
having enabled me to procure in Derge this rare collection of Tibetan prints
and for helping me in its preliminary classification. To Professor Dr. Lo-
kesh Chandra, Director of the International Academy of Indian Culture in
New Delhi, the author is indebted for a number of extremely helpful sug-
gestions and comments in the initial preparation of this edition. Last but
not least, my thanks are due to Venerable Champa N. Lodroe Dahortshang,
formerly of Sera monastery in Tibet and nowadays residing at the Tibet-
-Institut, Rikon (Switzerland) who afforded me numerous insights into parts
of this work which would otherwise have remained obscure.
Prague, February 1971
DR. JOSEF KOLMAŠ

("Great Corrector") Tshul-khrims-rin-chen. (1697-1769). 10 volumes, 174
items. -- Prague nos. 00.001-174 (Serial nos. 1-174).

reinafter referred to as IT). Cf. also Selected Writings of Zhu.chen
Tshul-khrims Rin-chen, 3. vols, Announced to be in press by Ngawang
Gyaltsan (Dehra Dun, India), and to be completed by January 1971

II. RGYUD SDE KUN BTUS (pp. 80- 128)
Collection of various Tantric texts; a Sa-skya-pa work by different authors.
According to E. Gene Smith, University of Washington Tibetan Catalogue,
Part I (University of Washington, Seattle 1969), vol. 2, p. 205 (hereinafter
referred to as UWTC), the compilation of this important collection of works
and commentarie's dealing with the so-called "new" tantras (rgyud gsar-ma)
was initiated by 'Jam-dbyangs mkhyen-brtse'i-dbang-po Kun-dga'-bstan-pa'i
-rgyal-mtshan (1820–92), but the major part of the work was performed by
his student, Ngor-pa dpon-slob 'Jam-dbyangs Blo-gter-dbang-po (ca. 1856 -
ca. 1914). 32 volumes, 242 items. -- Prague nos. 01.001–242 (Serial nos.
175-416).
Ref.: IT p. 508, no. 4. See also R.-A. Stein, Recherches sur l'épopée et
le barde au Tibet, Paris 1959, p. 39 (hereinafter referred to as REBT).
N. Gyaltsan (vide supra) announced his 30-volume reprint edition of
rGyud sde kun btus to be completed by July 1971.

III. SGRUB THABS KUN BTUS (pp. 129–199)
Collection of sadhanas and related texts of the Vajrayāna tradition of Tibet,
by different authors. According to Lokesh Chandra's Materials for a Hi-
story of Tibetan Literature (hereinafter referred to as MHTL), vol. 3, p.
15, the compiler of this tremendous sādhana collectaneum was 'Jam-
-dbyangs mkhyen-brtse'i-dbang-po Kun-dga'-bstan-pa'i-rgyal-mtshan, the
famous Sa-skya-pa monk, who was born in 1820 in a district in mDo-khams
and passed away in 1892 in Derge. 14 volumes, 353 items (item 02.121
missing). -- Prague nos. 02.001--354 (Serial nos, 417–769).
Ref.: IT p. 508, no. 8. MHTL III, pp. 15 and 697-715 where the list of 396
sadhana titles is reproduced (nos. 16538–16933). In 1970, Mr. Nga-
wang Gyaltsan of Dehra Dun, India, announced a 14-Volume edition
of sGrub thabs kun btus to be completed by August, 1970 (not seen).

XVIII. SA SKYA'I GSUNG 'BUM (pp. 466-493)
Complete works of the Five Masters of the Sa-skya-pa order, . viz. two
volumes (A 1-2) of writings of Sa-chen Kun-dga'-sňing-po (1092-1158);
three volumes (B 3-5) of writings of his son, Slob-dpon Rin-po-che bSod-
-nams-itse-mo (1142–82); four volumes: (C 6–9) of writings of another.
of his sons, rje-btsun Rin-po-che Grags-pa-rgyal-mtshan (1147–1216); three
volumes (D 10–12) of writings of Chos-rje Sa-skya pandita Kun-dga'-rgyal-
-mtshan (1182-1251); and finally, three volumes (E 13-15) of writings of his
nephew, 'Phags-pa Blo-gros-rgyal-mtshan (1235-80). 15 volumes, 136 items.
-- Prague nos. 17.001-136 (Serial nos. 2048–2183).
Ref.: IT p. 508, no. 6. The text of the sa-skya'i gsung-'bum (or, Sa-skya-pa'i
bka'-'bum), totalling 682 items in fifteen volumes (KA-BA), is now
easily available in a handy Japanese reprint prepared by the Toyo
Bunko and entitled The Complete Works of the Great Masters of the
Sa skya Sect of the Tibetan Buddhism, Tokyo 1968 (Bibliotheca
Tibetica I, vols. 1-7).
